to ehen, the\r\nequivalent of \xe2\x80\x9cno;\xe2\x80\x9d theu is \xe2\x80\x9cship\xe2\x80\x9d in the above, and \xe2\x80\x9csnow\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nin the Italian; courire (the equivalent of covrire or coprire, \xe2\x80\x9cto\r\ncover\xe2\x80\x9d) in the Italian, becomes courir (\xe2\x80\x9cto run\xe2\x80\x9d) in MS. 5,650.\r\nAll are to be regarded as errors of the French. Certain words are left\r\nin Italian in MS. 5,650, which are as follows: la copa; alcalcagno;\r\n(Italian MS. al calcagno); homo squerzo (Italian MS. sguerco); a la\r\npignate (Italian MS. pigniata); alstruzzo vcelo (Italian MS. al seruzo\r\nucelo); and alcocinare (Italian MS. al co\xc3\xa7inare). Stanley offers this\r\nas proof that MS. 5,650 was written by Pigafetta, and not translated\r\nfrom his Italian, but it furnishes no evidence that Pigafetta even saw\r\nthe French version of his relation. It must be remembered that Stanley\r\ndid not himself see the Italian MS. but only the Amoretti mutilation of\r\nit (from which, and from MS. 5,650, he reproduces the vocabulary,\r\nwithout English translation); and hence bases his observations on that\r\nand the conjectures of its editor. Stanley points out the fact that\r\nAmoretti has omitted several words of this list, but they are all in\r\nthe Italian MS. A sad blunder has been made by Stanley in his\r\ntranscription of La pouldre dherbe qui mangent whose Patagonian\r\nequivalent is capac. He transcribes as follows: la pouldre d\xe2\x80\x99herbe\r\nwith Patagonian equivalent qui (which it is to be noted is only the\r\nwrong form of the French relative), and mangent with Patagonian\r\nequivalent capac, explaining mangent in a footnote as \xe2\x80\x9cFood, the root\r\nused as bread.\xe2\x80\x9d Stanley also makes the following mistranscriptions:\r\norescho for oresche (\xe2\x80\x9cnostrils\xe2\x80\x9d); canneghin for caimeghin (\xe2\x80\x9cpalm\r\nof the hand\xe2\x80\x9d); ochy for ochii (\xe2\x80\x9cbosom\xe2\x80\x9d); scancos for scaneos\r\n(\xe2\x80\x9ctesticles\xe2\x80\x9d); hou for hoii (\xe2\x80\x9cbuttocks\xe2\x80\x9d); ohoy for ohon\r\n(\xe2\x80\x9cpulse\xe2\x80\x9d); cartschem for cartscheni (\xe2\x80\x9csole of the foot\xe2\x80\x9d); chol\r\nfor thol (\xe2\x80\x9cheart\xe2\x80\x9d); om for oni (\xe2\x80\x9cwind\xe2\x80\x9d); aschame for aschanie\r\n(\xe2\x80\x9cearthen pot\xe2\x80\x9d); oamaghei for oamaghce (\xe2\x80\x9cto fight\xe2\x80\x9d); amet for\r\namel (\xe2\x80\x9cblack\xe2\x80\x9d); and ixecoles for jrocoles (\xe2\x80\x9cto cook\xe2\x80\x9d). Amoretti\r\nhas also made many errors (see Stanley\xe2\x80\x99s First Voyage, pp. 62, 63).\r\nMosto, who is on the whole a faithful transcriber, has sacancos as the\r\nPatagonian equivalent of a li testiculi; om jani for a li sui, the\r\ncorrect forms of the latter being jani and a li sui oui; and tcrechai\r\nfor the equivalent of \xe2\x80\x9cred cloth.\xe2\x80\x9d Eden (p. 252) gives only the\r\nfollowing words: \xe2\x80\x9cbreade, Capar: water, Oli: redde clothe, Cherecai:\r\nred colour, Cheiche: blacke colour, Amel.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMosto (p. 63, note 8) gives the following words from the vocabulary of\r\nthe Tehuel-ches compiled by the second lieutenant of the ship\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cRoncagli,\xe2\x80\x9d which correspond almost exactly with those given by\r\nPigafetta.\r\n\r\n\r\n             English   Roncagli   Pigafetta\r\n             Nose      or         or\r\n             eye       \xc3\xb3thel      other\r\n             hand      tz\xc3\xa9n       chene\r\n             ear       sha        sane\r\n             ostrich   \xc3\xb3yue       hoi hoi\r\n\r\n\r\nBrinton (American Race, p. 328) cites Ramon Lista (Mis exploraciones y\r\ndescubr\xc3\xacmientos en Patagonia, Buenos Ayres, 1880) in proof that the\r\nlanguage of the Patagonians has undergone but slight change since the\r\ntime of Pigafetta. See also lists of words in Brinton (ut supra), p.\r\n364, from the Patagonian and Fuegian languages. The vocabularies given\r\nby Horatio Hale (Wilkes\xe2\x80\x99s U. S. Exploring Expedition, 1838\xe2\x80\x931842,\r\nPhiladelphia, 1846, viii, pp. 651\xe2\x80\x93656) bear no resemblance to\r\nPigafetta\xe2\x80\x99s vocabulary. Hale says that guttural sounds are frequent\r\namong the Indians of the Patagonian district.\r\n\r\n[162] MS. 5,650 reads: \xe2\x80\x9ccapae.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n[163] Cf. with the methods of fire-making used by the North American\r\nIndians in Jesuit Relations and Allied Documents (Cleveland reissue);\r\nsee also Captivity of Hans Stade (Hakluyt Society edition), p. 126.\r\n\r\nAt this point (folio 14a) in the original Italian MS. occurs the first\r\nchart, representing the straits of Magellan (see p. 86). The cardinal\r\npoints in all of Pigafetta\xe2\x80\x99s charts are the reverse of the ordinary,\r\nthe north being below and the south above. MS. 5,650 precedes this\r\nchart (which there occupies folio 21a) by the words: \xe2\x80\x9cBelow is\r\ndepicted the strait of Patagonie.\xe2\x80\x9d Immediately following this chart\r\nin the Italian MS. (folio 15a) is the chart of the Ysole Infortunate\r\n(\xe2\x80\x9cUnfortunate Isles;\xe2\x80\x9d see p. 92). These islands are shown in MS.\r\n5,650 on folio 23a, with the following notice: \xe2\x80\x9cHere are shown the\r\ntwo islands called \xe2\x80\x98Unfortunate Islands.\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d The charts in the\r\nItalian MS. are brown or dull black on a blue ground.\r\n\r\n[164] The \xe2\x80\x9cRoteiro\xe2\x80\x9d (Stanley, p. 9) says that Magalh\xc3\xa3es left the\r\nstrait November 26 (having entered it October 21); the anonymous\r\nPortuguese (Stanley, p. 31) and Peter Martyr (Mosto, p. 65, note 1),\r\nNovember 27.\r\n\r\n[165] MS. 5,650 reads: \xe2\x80\x9cAnd we ate only biscuits that had fallen to\r\npowder, which was quite full of worms, and stank from the filth of the\r\nurine of rats that covered it, and of which the good had been eaten.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nEden (p. 252) reads: \xe2\x80\x9cAnd hauynge in this tyme con\xc5\xbfumed all theyr\r\nby\xc5\xbfket and other vyttales, they fell into \xc5\xbfuche nece\xc5\xbf\xc5\xbfitie that\r\nthey were inforced to eate the pouder that remayned therof beinge nowe\r\nfull of woormes and \xc5\xbftynkynge lyke py\xc5\xbf\xc5\xbfe by rea\xc5\xbfon of the \xc5\xbfalte\r\nwater,\xe2\x80\x9d Herrera (Navarrete, iv, p. 51) says that the rice was cooked\r\nwith salt water.\r\n\r\n[166] A curious coincidence in view of Magalh\xc3\xa3es\xe2\x80\x99s answer to Esteban\r\nGomez at a council called in the strait to discuss the continuance of\r\nthe voyage that \xe2\x80\x9calthough he had to eat the cowhide wrappings of the\r\nyardarms, he would still persevere and discover what he had promised\r\nthe emperor\xe2\x80\x9d (Navarrete, iv, p. 43; cited from Herrera). At that\r\ncouncil Andr\xc3\xa9 de San Martin, pilot in the \xe2\x80\x9cSan Antonio,\xe2\x80\x9d advised\r\nthat they continue explorations until the middle of January, 1521, and\r\nthen return to Spain; and urged that no farther southward descent be\r\nmade, and that navigation along so dangerous coasts be only by day, in\r\norder that the crew might have some rest (Navarrete, iv, pp. 45\xe2\x80\x9349).\r\n\r\n[167] MS. 5,650 reads: \xe2\x80\x9cenough of them.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n[168] This was the scurvy. Navarrete (iv, p. 54) following a document\r\nconserved in Archivo general de Indias, says that only eleven men died\r\nof scurvy during the voyage from the strait to the Ladrones.\r\n\r\n[169] The anonymous Portuguese says (Stanley, p. 31) that after sailing\r\nwest and northwest for 9,858 miles, the equator was reached. At the\r\nline (\xe2\x80\x9cRoteiro,\xe2\x80\x9d Stanley, p. 9), Magalh\xc3\xa3es changed the course in\r\norder to strike land north of the Moluccas, as \xe2\x80\x9che had information\r\nthat there were no provisions\xe2\x80\x9d there.\r\n\r\n[170] MS. 5,650 reads: \xe2\x80\x9cIt is well named Pacific.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n[171] MS. 5,650 adds: \xe2\x80\x9cwhich is a large fish called tiburoni.\xe2\x80\x9d The\r\nanonymous Portuguese (Stanley, p. 31), says that the Unfortunate\r\nIslands were met before the line was reached and were eight hundred\r\nmiles distant from one another. One was called St. Peter (in 18\xc2\xb0) and\r\nthe other the island of Tiburones (in 14\xc2\xb0). Transylvanus (Vol. I, p.\r\n321), Herrera, and Oviedo, say that the three vessels stopped two days\r\nat those islands for supplies, but Albo\xe2\x80\x99s journal (Navarrete, iv, p.\r\n218) indicates that no stop was made there. The \xe2\x80\x9cRoteiro\xe2\x80\x9d (Stanley,\r\np. 9), gives the latitude of these islands as 18\xc2\xb0 or 19\xc2\xb0 and 13\xc2\xb0 or\r\n14\xc2\xb0. Albo (Navarrete, iv, p. 218) says that the first was discovered\r\nJanuary 24 in 16\xc2\xb0 15\xc2\xb4, and was called San Pablo, because that was the\r\ndate of St. Paul\xe2\x80\x99s conversion; and the island of Tiburones was\r\ndiscovered February 4, in 10\xc2\xb0 40\xc2\xb4, at a distance of 9\xc2\xb0 (sic) from\r\nthe former. Eden (p. 253) says that the second island lay in 5\xc2\xb0. These\r\ntwo islands were probably Puka-puka (the Honden Eyland of the Dutch\r\natlases) of the Tuamotu group, located in latitude 14\xc2\xb0 45\xc2\xb4 south, and\r\nlongitude 138\xc2\xb0 48\xc2\xb4 west; and Flint Island of the Manihiki group,\r\nlocated in latitude 11\xc2\xb0 20\xc2\xb4 south and longitude 151\xc2\xb0 48\xc2\xb4 west. The\r\nlatter is still uninhabited, but the former contains a population of\r\nover four hundred. See ante, note 163. See Guillemard, p. 220, and\r\nMosto, p. 65, note 6.\r\n\r\n[172] MS. 5,650 reads: \xe2\x80\x9cnow at the stern, now at the windward side,\r\nor otherwise.\xe2\x80\x9d Amoretti changes this passage completely, reading:\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAccording to our measurement of the distance that we made with the\r\nchain astern, we ran from sixty to seventy leagues daily.\xe2\x80\x9d Many\r\nbasing themselves on this passage of Amoretti, have believed that the\r\nlog was in use at the time of the first circumnavigation. Dr. Breusing\r\n(Die Catena a poppa bei Pigafetta und die Logge, in \xe2\x80\x9cZeitschrift der\r\nGesellschaft f\xc3\xbcr Erdkunde zu Berlin,\xe2\x80\x9d 1869, iv, pp. 107\xe2\x80\x93115)\r\nbelieves that the \xe2\x80\x9cstern chain (catena poppa) is not the log properly\r\nso-called, but an instrument for determining the angle of the ship\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nleeway, an opinion accepted also by Gelcich in his La scoperta\r\nd\xe2\x80\x99America e Cristoforo Colombo nella letteratura moderna (Gorizia,\r\n1890). L\xe2\x80\x99Vzielle (Studi bibliogr. e biogr. sulla storia della geogr.\r\nin Italia, Roma, 1875, part ii, introduction, pp. 294\xe2\x80\x93296), combats\r\nthat opinion, as well as the idea that the log is meant. The difficulty\r\nof the passage, he says, hinges on the word ho and whether it is\r\ninterpreted as a verb or a conjunction. If it be a conjunction then the\r\npassage, means \xe2\x80\x9cestimating by sight, the rate of the ship from the\r\n\xe2\x80\x98bow catena,\xe2\x80\x99 or at the stern\xe2\x80\x9d (\xe2\x80\x98catena\xe2\x80\x99 being a beam\r\nperpendicular to the ship\xe2\x80\x99s axis at the point near the bow where it\r\nbegins to curve inward; that is, at such a point that from that place\r\nto the stern, the direction of the apparent way is parallel to the\r\nlongitudinal axis of the ship) his ship made fifty, sixty, or seventy\r\nleagues.\xe2\x80\x9d One might suppose, if ho be regarded as a verb, that\r\nPigafetta called catena a cross beam of the stern (the passage reading\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cthe catena that was at the stern\xe2\x80\x9d); or that the disjunctive ho,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cor\xe2\x80\x9d is used in place of e, \xe2\x80\x9cand,\xe2\x80\x9d and that Pigafetta, dividing\r\nthe distance between the stern and the bow catena by the time necessary\r\nfor a fixed point of the sea to pass from the elevation of the bow to\r\nthat of the stern, thus deduced the ship\xe2\x80\x99s rate. See Mosto, p. 66,\r\nnote 1. L\xe2\x80\x99Vzielli\xe2\x80\x99s opinion is the most probable, for although the\r\nlog is mentioned by Purchas as early as 1607, its use did not become\r\ngeneral until 1620. An instrument used to measure the rates of vessels\r\nis mentioned as early as 1577, but it was very deficient.\r\n\r\n[173] The \xe2\x80\x9cRoteiro\xe2\x80\x9d (Stanley, p. 6) says that this cape, which he\r\ncalls \xe2\x80\x9ccape of the virgins\xe2\x80\x9d was discovered on October 21, 1520, and\r\nlay in latitude about 52\xc2\xb0 south. Barros says that it was discovered on\r\nOctober 20; and Transylvanus and Oviedo, on November 27. See Mosto, p.\r\n61, note 1.\r\n\r\n[174] Regarding the reckonings Eden says: \xe2\x80\x9cIn \xc5\xbfo much that it was\r\nnece\xc5\xbf\xc5\xbfarie to helpe the needle with the lode \xc5\xbftone (commonly cauled\r\nthe adamant) before they could \xc5\xbfaile therwith, bycau\xc5\xbfe it moued not\r\nas it doothe when it is in the\xc5\xbfe owre partes.\xe2\x80\x9d Eden also gives a cut\r\nof the \xe2\x80\x9c\xc5\xbftarres abowt the pole Antartike.\xe2\x80\x9d The same author also\r\n(pp. 277\xe2\x80\x93280) compiles from Amerigo Vespucci and Andreas de Corsali a\r\ntreatise entitled \xe2\x80\x9cOf the Pole Antartike and the stars abowt the same\r\nand of the qualitie of the regions and disposition of the Elementes\r\nabowt the Equinoctiall line. Al\xc5\xbfo certeyne \xc5\xbfecreates touching the\r\narte of \xc5\xbfaylynge.\xe2\x80\x9d The former says: \xe2\x80\x9cThe pole Antartike hath\r\nnother the great beare nor the lyttle as is \xc5\xbfeene abowte owre pole.\r\nBut hath foure \xc5\xbftarres whiche compa\xc5\xbf\xc5\xbfe it abowt in forme of a\r\nquadrangle. When these are hydden, there is \xc5\xbfeene on the lefte \xc5\xbfyde a\r\nbryght Canopus of three \xc5\xbftarres of notable greatne\xc5\xbf\xc5\xbfe, whiche beinge\r\nin the mydde\xc5\xbft of heauen, repre\xc5\xbfenteth this figure.\xe2\x80\x9d The latter\r\nsays: \xe2\x80\x9cHere we \xc5\xbfawe a marueylous order of \xc5\xbftarres, \xc5\xbfo that in the\r\nparte of heauen contrary to owre northe pole, to knowe in what place\r\nand degree the \xc5\xbfouth pole was, we tooke the day with the \xc5\xbfoonne, and\r\nob\xc5\xbferued the nyght with the a\xc5\xbftrolabie, and \xc5\xbfaw manife\xc5\xbftly twoo\r\nclowdes of rea\xc5\xbfonable bygne\xc5\xbf\xc5\xbfe mouynge abowt the place of the pole\r\ncontinually nowe ry\xc5\xbfynge and nowe faulynge, \xc5\xbfo keepynge theyr\r\ncontinuall cour\xc5\xbfe in circular mouynge, with a \xc5\xbftarre euer in the\r\nmydde\xc5\xbft which is turned abowt with them abowte. xi. degrees frome the\r\npole. Aboue the\xc5\xbfe appeareth a marueylous cro\xc5\xbf\xc5\xbfe in the mydde\xc5\xbft of\r\nfyue notable \xc5\xbftarres which compa\xc5\xbf\xc5\xbfe it abowt.... This cro\xc5\xbf\xc5\xbfe is so\r\nfayre and bewtiful, that none other heuenly gne may be compared to\r\nit....\xe2\x80\x9d These are the Magallanic clouds (Nuebecula major and Nubecula\r\nminor) and the constellation of the Southern Cross or Crux. The\r\nMagellanic clouds resemble portions of the milky way, Nubecula major\r\nbeing visible to the naked eye in strong moonlight and covering about\r\ntwo hundred times the moon\xe2\x80\x99s surface, while the Nubecula minor,\r\nalthough visible to the naked eye, disappears in full moonlight, and\r\ncovers an area only one-fourth that of the former. They were first\r\nobserved by the Arabians. The Portuguese pilots probably called them at\r\nfirst \xe2\x80\x9cclouds of the cape.\xe2\x80\x9d (Mosto, p. 66, note 2). The Southern\r\nCross, which resembles a lute rather than a cross, was first erected\r\ninto a constellation by Royer in 1679, although often spoken of before\r\nas a cross. Only one of its five principal stars belongs to the first\r\nmagnitude. The cross is only 6\xc2\xb0 in extent north and south and less\r\nthan that east and west.\r\n\r\nThe second chart of the plate at p. 92 represents the Ladrones Islands\r\nand occurs in the Italian MS. at this point (folio 16b). This chart is\r\nfound on folio 25b in MS. 5,650, and is preceded by the inscription:\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe island of the robbers and the style of their boats.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n[175] MS. 5,650 reads: \xe2\x80\x9cDuring that time of two months and twelve\r\ndays.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n[176] Amoretti reads: \xe2\x80\x9cthree degrees east of Capo Verde.\xe2\x80\x9d If the\r\ncape is meant, the correction is proper, but if the islands, the MS. is\r\ncorrect. See Mosto, p. 67, note 4.\r\n\r\n[177] Cipangu is Japan, while Sumbdit Pradit may be the island of\r\nAntilia, called \xe2\x80\x9cSepte citade\xe2\x80\x9d on Martin Behaim\xe2\x80\x99s globe (Mosto,\r\np. 67, note 5). The locations given by Pigafetta prove that they did\r\nnot see them, but that he writes only from vague reports. Europe first\r\nlearned of Japan, near the end of the thirteenth century, through Marco\r\nPolo, who had been told in China fabulous tales of the wealth of\r\nZipangu. This word is derived by Marco Polo from the Chinese\r\nDschi-pen-Ku\xc3\xab or Dschi-pon, which the Japanese have transformed into\r\nNippon or Nihon. See Travels of Marco Polo, book iii, ch. ii; and\r\nRein\xe2\x80\x99s Japan, p. 4.\r\n\r\n[178] See Vol. I, pp. 208, 209, 210, 312, 336.\r\n\r\n[179] MS. 5,650 reads: \xe2\x80\x9csixty.\xe2\x80\x9d Transylvanus (Vol. I, p. 322) names\r\ntwo islands of the Ladrones Inuagana and Acacan, but says that both\r\nwere uninhabited. The\r\nanonymous Portuguese (Stanley, p. 31) says that the Ladrones (which lay\r\nin 10\xc2\xb0\xe2\x80\x9312\xc2\xb0 north latitude, were 2,046 miles by the course traveled\r\nfrom the equator. Brito (Navarrete, iv, p. 308) says: \xe2\x80\x9cThence [i.e.,\r\nthe Unfortunate Islands] they laid their course westward, and after\r\nsailing 500 leguas came to certain islands where they found a\r\nconsiderable number of savages. So many of the latter boarded the\r\nvessels that when the men tried to restore order in them, they were\r\nunable to get rid of the savages except by lance-thrusts. They killed\r\nmany savages, who laughed as if it were a cause for rejoicing.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n[186] MS. 5,650 adds: \xe2\x80\x9cor superior.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n[187] MS. 5,650 reads: \xe2\x80\x9ccloth.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n[188] At this point, MS. 5,650 begins a new sentence, thus: \xe2\x80\x9cThere\r\nare found in that place.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n[189] MS. 5,650 reads: \xe2\x80\x9cThose women.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n[190] MS. 5,650 makes use of the Italian word store for stuoje or stoje\r\nmeaning \xe2\x80\x9cmats,\xe2\x80\x9d and explains by adding: \xe2\x80\x9cwhich we call mats.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n[191] They also (according to Herrera) received the name Las Velas,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cthe sails\xe2\x80\x9d from the lateen-rigged vessels that the natives used\r\n(Mosto, p. 67, note 7). See also Vol. XVI, pp. 200\xe2\x80\x93202.\r\n\r\n[192] In MS. 5,650 this sentence reads as follows: \xe2\x80\x9cThe pastime of\r\nthe men and women of the said place and their sport, is to go in their\r\nboats to catch those flying fish with fishhooks made of fishbone.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n[193] Mosto (p. 68, note 5) says that these boats were the fisolere,\r\nwhich were small and very swift oared-vessels, used in winter on the\r\nVenetian lakes by the Venetian nobles for hunting with bows and arrows\r\nand guns. Amoretti conjectures that Pigafetta means the fusiniere,\r\nboats named after Fusine whence people are ferried to Venice.\r\n\r\n[194] MS. 5,650 reads: \xe2\x80\x9cThe said boats have no difference between\r\nstern and bow.\xe2\x80\x9d Albo (Navarrete, iv, p. 219), in speaking of the\r\nboats of the Chamorros, uses almost identically the same expression:\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey went both ways, for they could make the stern, bow, and the\r\nbow, stern, whenever they wished.\xe2\x80\x9d The apparatus described by\r\nPigafetta as belonging to these boats is the outrigger, common to many\r\nof the boats of the eastern islands.\r\n\r\n[195] In the Italian MS., the chart of Aguada ly boni segnaly\r\n(\xe2\x80\x9cWatering-place of good signs\xe2\x80\x9d), Zzamal (Samar), Abarien, Humunu,\r\nHyunagan, Zuluam, Cenalo, and Ybusson (q.v., p. 102) follows at this\r\npoint. It is found on folio 29b of MS. 5,650 and is preceded by the\r\nfollowing: \xe2\x80\x9cHere is shown the island of Good Signs, and the four\r\nislands, Cenalo, Humanghar, Ibusson, and Abarien, and several\r\nothers.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n[196] \xe2\x80\x9cThe tenth of March\xe2\x80\x9d in Eden, and the distance of Zamal from\r\nthe Ladrones is given as \xe2\x80\x9cxxx. leagues.\xe2\x80\x9d Albo (Navarrete, iv, p.\r\n220) says that the first land seen was called Yunagan, \xe2\x80\x9cwhich\r\nextended north and had many bays;\xe2\x80\x9d and that going south from there\r\nthey anchored at a small island called Suluan. At the former \xe2\x80\x9cwe saw\r\nsome canoes, and went thither, but they fled. That island lies in 9\xc2\xb0\r\n40\xc2\xb4 north latitude.\xe2\x80\x9d The \xe2\x80\x9cRoteiro\xe2\x80\x9d (Stanley, p. 10) says that\r\nthe first land seen was in \xe2\x80\x9cbarely eleven degrees,\xe2\x80\x9d and that the\r\nfleet \xe2\x80\x9cwent to touch at another further on, which appeared first.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nTwo praus approached a boat sent ashore, whereupon the latter was\r\nordered back, and the praus fled. Thereupon the fleet went to another\r\nnearby island \xe2\x80\x9cwhich lies in ten degrees, to which they gave the name\r\nof the \xe2\x80\x98Island of Good Signs,\xe2\x80\x99 because they found some gold in\r\nit.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n[197] This word is omitted in MS. 5,650.\r\n\r\n[198] MS. 5,650 reads: \xe2\x80\x9cmore than one foot long.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n[199] Since rice is an important staple among all the eastern islands,\r\nit is natural that there are different and distinctive names for that\r\ngrain in the various languages and dialects for all stages of its\r\ngrowth and all its modes of preparation. Thus the Tag\xc3\xa1log has words\r\nfor \xe2\x80\x9cgreen rice,\xe2\x80\x9d \xe2\x80\x9crice with small heads,\xe2\x80\x9d \xe2\x80\x9cdirty and partly\r\nrotten rice,\xe2\x80\x9d \xe2\x80\x9cearly rice,\xe2\x80\x9d \xe2\x80\x9clate rice,\xe2\x80\x9d \xe2\x80\x9ccooked rice,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nand many others. Mosto, citing Boerio (Dizion.\r\nveneziano), says of rizali: \xe2\x80\x9cRizzagio or rizzagno, \xe2\x80\x98sweepnet\xe2\x80\x99 a\r\nfine thickly woven net, which when thrown into rivers by the fisherman,\r\nopens, and when near the bottom, closes, and covers and encloses the\r\nfish. Rizzagio is also called that contrivance or net, made in the\r\nmanner of an inverted cone, with a barrel hoop attached to the\r\ncircumference as a selvage. It has a hole underneath, through which if\r\nthe eels in the ponds slyly enter the net, there is no danger of their\r\nescape.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nFish are caught in the Philippines by various devices\xe2\x80\x94in favorable\r\nsituations by traps, weirs, corrals of bamboo set along the shore in\r\nshallow waters. Various kinds of nets and seines, the hook and line,\r\nand also the spear, are also used. See Census of the Philippine Islands\r\n(Washington, 1905), iv, p. 533.\r\n\r\n[218] MS. 5,650 reads: \xe2\x80\x9cHiunanghar.\xe2\x80\x9d Stanley has mistranscribed\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHuinanghar.\xe2\x80\x9d It is difficult to identify the four islands of\r\nCenalo, Hiunanghan, Ibusson, and Abarien with certainty. Next vnto Flauius, the chiefe commendation is dew to the\r\nSpanyardes and Portugales by who\xc5\xbfe daylye experience, the \xc5\xbfame is\r\nbrought to further perfection, and the v\xc5\xbfe thereof better knowen;\r\nalthowghe hytherto no man knoweth the cau\xc5\xbfe why the iren touched with\r\nthe lode \xc5\xbftone, turneth euer towarde the north \xc5\xbftarre, as playnely\r\nappeareth in euery common dyall.\xe2\x80\x9d He also says: \xe2\x80\x9cAs touchynge the\r\nneedle of the compa\xc5\xbf\xc5\xbfe, I haue redde in the Portugales nauigations\r\nthat \xc5\xbfaylynge as farre \xc5\xbfouth as Cap. de Bona Speranza, the poynt of\r\nthe needle \xc5\xbftyll re\xc5\xbfpected the northe as it dyd on this \xc5\xbfyde the\r\nEquinoctiall, \xc5\xbfauynge that it \xc5\xbfumwhat trembeled and declyned a\r\nlyttle, whereby the force \xc5\xbfeemed \xc5\xbfumwhat to be dimini\xc5\xbf\xc5\xbfhed, \xc5\xbfo\r\nthat they were fayne to helpe it with the lode \xc5\xbftone.\xe2\x80\x9d (See ante, p.\r\n93). The compass was known in a rough form to the Chinese at early as\r\n2634 B.C., and first applied to navigation in the third or fourth\r\ncentury A.D., or perhaps earlier. It was probably introduced into\r\nEurope through the Arabs who learned of it from the Chinese. It is\r\nfirst referred to in European literature by Alexander Neckam in the\r\ntwelfth century in De Utensilibus. The variations from the true north\r\nwere observed as early as 1269.\r\n\r\n[230] Stanley says that the Amoretti edition represents the king as\r\nmaking this request and Magalh\xc3\xa3es as assenting thereto; but the\r\nItalian MS. reads as distinctly as MS. 5,650, that Magalh\xc3\xa3es made the\r\nrequest.\r\n\r\n[231] MS. 5,650 omits the remainder of this sentence.\r\n\r\n[232] MS. 5,650 adds: \xe2\x80\x9cthat is, a boat.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n[233] The following passage relating to the meal reads thus in MS.\r\n5,650: \xe2\x80\x9cThen the king had a plate of pork and some wine brought in.\r\nTheir fashion of drinking is as follows. He promised the king to treat those pilots as he would them\r\nthemselves, and that he would leave one of his men as a hostage. In\r\nreply the first king said that he would go himself to guide the captain\r\nto those ports and that he would be his pilot, but asked him to wait\r\ntwo days until he should gather his rice, and do some other things\r\nwhich he had to do. He asked the captain to lend him some of his men,\r\nso that he could accomplish it sooner, and the captain agreed to it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nAt this point MS. 5,650 begins a new unnumbered chapter.\r\n\r\n[254] The billon and afterward copper coin quattrino, which was struck\r\nin the mints of Venice, Rome, Florence, Reggio, the Two Sicilies, etc.\r\nThe quattrino of the popes was often distinguished as \xe2\x80\x9cquattrino\r\nRomano.\xe2\x80\x9d The Venetian copper quattrino was first struck in the reign\r\nof Francesco Foscari (1423\xe2\x80\x9357). See chart of these islands on p. 112.\r\n\r\nAlbo (Navarrete, iv, pp. 220, 221) says: \xe2\x80\x9cWe left Mazava and went\r\nnorth toward the island of Seilani, after which we ran along the said\r\nisland to the northwest as far as 10 degrees. There we saw three rocky\r\nislands, and turned our course west for about 10 leguas where we came\r\nupon two islets. We stayed there that night and in the morning went\r\ntoward the south southwest for about 12 leguas, as far as 10 and\r\none-third degrees. At that point we entered a channel between two\r\nislands, one of which is called Matan and the other Subu. Subu, as well\r\nas the islands of Mazava and Suluan extend north by east and south by\r\nwest. Between Subu and Seilani we spied a very lofty land lying to the\r\nnorth, which is called Baibai. It is said to contain considerable gold\r\nand to be well stocked with food, and so great an extent of land that\r\nits limits are unknown. From Mazava, Seilani, and Subu, on the course\r\nfollowed toward the south, look out for the many shoals, which are very\r\nbad. On that account a canoe which was guiding us along that course,\r\nrefused to go ahead. From the beginning of the channel of Subu and\r\nMatan, we turned west by a middle channel and reached the city of Subu.\r\nThere we anchored and made peace, and the people there gave us rice,\r\nmillet, and meat. We stayed there for a considerable time. The king and\r\nqueen of that place and many of the inhabitants readily became\r\nChristians.\xe2\x80\x9d The \xe2\x80\x9cRoteiro\xe2\x80\x9d (Stanley, p. 11) says that the king of\r\nMacangar (i.e., Mazaua) conducted the Spaniards \xe2\x80\x9ca matter of thirty\r\nleagues to another island named Cabo [i.e., Ceb\xc3\xba], which is in ten\r\ndegrees, and in this island Fernando de Magalh\xc3\xa3es did what he pleased\r\nwith the consent of the country.\xe2\x80\x9d Brito says merely (Navarrete, iv,\r\np. 308): \xe2\x80\x9cAfter that, after passing amid many islands, they reached\r\none called Mazaba, which lies in 9 degrees. The king of Mazaba\r\nconducted them to another large island called Zub\xc3\xb3.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n[266] MS. 5,650 reads: \xe2\x80\x9conly one of them.\xe2\x80\x9d Barbastili is a Venetian\r\nword for pipistrelli. These bats are the Pteropi or \xe2\x80\x9cflying foxes,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nthe large fruit-eating bats of which so many species inhabit the Malay\r\nArchipelago. Bats are especially found in Guimar\xc3\xa1s, Siquijor, and\r\nCeb\xc3\xba, and the skins of some are used as fur. See Guillemard (ut supra,\r\np. 235). See also Delgado\xe2\x80\x99s Historia, pp. 842, 843; and U. S.\r\nPhilippine Gazetteer.\r\n\r\n[267] Stanley mistranslates as \xe2\x80\x9ctortoises.\xe2\x80\x9d The \xe2\x80\x9cblack birds with\r\nthe long tail\xe2\x80\x9d are the tab\xc3\xb3n \xe2\x80\x9cmound-building Megapodes,\r\ngallinacious birds peculiar to the Austro-Malayan subregion\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n(Guillemard\xe2\x80\x99s Magellan, p. 235). See Pigafetta\xe2\x80\x99s chart showing these\r\nislands on p. 112.\r\n\r\n[269] Following this point in the Italian MS. (folio 26a) is the chart\r\nof the islands of Bohol, Mattam, and Zzubu (q.v., p. 136).\r\n\r\nMS. 5,650 presents this chart on folio 51a, preceded by the words:\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBelow are shown the islands of Zzubu, Mattan, and Boh\xc3\xb3l.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n[270] MS. 5,650 reads: \xe2\x80\x9cBut the interpreter reassured them by telling\r\nthem.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n[271] MS. 5,650 reads: \xe2\x80\x9cand he was going, by the orders of the said\r\nsovereign, to discover the islands of Mallucque.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n[272] MS. 5,650 reads: \xe2\x80\x9cThereupon the abovesaid merchant said to the\r\nking in their language,\xe2\x80\x9d etc., without giving the original Malay\r\nwords. Eden gives the phrase as catacaia chita.\r\n\r\n[273] Calicut, properly K\xc3\xa1l\xc3\xadkot (said to be derived from two words\r\nmeaning cock-crow, because the territory granted to the first king of\r\nK\xc3\xa1l\xc3\xadkot was limited to the extent over which a cock could be heard to\r\ncrow; or from K\xc3\xa1li, one of the names of the goddess Gauri) is the name\r\nof a district and city on the Malabar coast. The king of all the\r\nMalabar coast from Goa to Cape Comorin, Samari Perymal, having adopted\r\nthe Mahometan faith divided his kingdom into the kingdoms of Calicut,\r\nCochin, Cananor, and Coul\xc3\xa3o, and gave them to his friends, on\r\ncondition that the king of Calicut be termed \xe2\x80\x9cZamorim\xe2\x80\x9d or\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSamorim,\xe2\x80\x9d i.e., \xe2\x80\x9cSupreme emperor and God upon earth\xe2\x80\x9d (although\r\nthe proper form is said to be \xe2\x80\x9cTamurin\xe2\x80\x9d which is conjectured by\r\nsome to be a modification of the Sanskrit \xe2\x80\x9cSamunri,\xe2\x80\x9d \xe2\x80\x9cseaking.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nThe city of K\xc3\xa1l\xc3\xadkot, a noted emporium of trade, was built perhaps as\r\nearly as 805 A.D., although the date 1300 A.D. is also given as that of\r\nits founding; and is described by Ibn Batuta in 1342 as one of the\r\nfinest ports in the world. It was visited by Covilham in 1486, and\r\nVasco da Gama\xe2\x80\x99s ships were freighted there in 1498. The latter\r\nattacked the city in 1503 and 1510, and the Portuguese built a\r\nfortified factory there in 1513 which was destroyed by the governor in\r\n1525 to avoid its falling into the enemy\xe2\x80\x99s hands. The English\r\nestablished a factory in the city in 1616, which was captured in 1766\r\nby Haidar Ali; but after a further series of capture and recapture, the\r\ncity and district was permanently turned over to the British (1792).\r\nSee Stanley\xe2\x80\x99s Vasco da Gama (Hakluyt Society publications, London,\r\n1869); Birch\xe2\x80\x99s Alboquerque (Hakluyt Society publications, London,\r\n1875\xe2\x80\x931884); Jones and Badger\xe2\x80\x99s Ludovico di Varthema (Hakluyt\r\nSociety publications, London, 1863), pp. 135\xe2\x80\x93177; also Grey\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nTravels of Pietro della Valle (Hakluyt Society publications, London,\r\n1892), pp. 344, 345, note.\r\n\r\nMalacca, or more correctly M\xc3\xa2laka is the name of an ancient territory\r\nand city, which was probably first settled by Javanese, and is possibly\r\nderived from \xe2\x80\x9cMalayu\xe2\x80\x9d meaning in Javanese \xe2\x80\x9cto run\xe2\x80\x9d or\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cfugitive.\xe2\x80\x9d At an early period Malacca fell under the sway of the\r\nSiamese. The city, located on both sides of the Malacca River, and only\r\none hundred and thirty miles northwest of Singapore (which has usurped\r\nthe great volume of trade once centering at Malacca) was founded about\r\n1250 A.D. The first European to visit the city was Varthema, about the\r\nyear 1505. It was captured by the Portuguese under Albuquerque in 1511,\r\nand they held it (1580\xe2\x80\x931640 under Spanish control) until 1641 when it\r\nwas captured by the Dutch, who had unsuccessfully besieged it, with the\r\naid of the king of Jahor, in 1606. The English obtained possession of\r\nit in 1795, and still hold it, although the Dutch possessed it from\r\n1818\xe2\x80\x931825. For descriptions and history of Malacca, see the following\r\nHakluyt Society publications: Stanley\xe2\x80\x99s East Africa and Malabar\r\n(London, 1866), pp. 190\xe2\x80\x93195; Birch\xe2\x80\x99s Alboquerque, iii, pp. 71\xe2\x80\x9390\r\n(and other citations); Burnell and Tiele\xe2\x80\x99s Linschoten (London, 1885),\r\ni, pp. 104\xe2\x80\x93106; Gray\xe2\x80\x99s Voyage of Fran\xc3\xa7ois Pyrard (London, 1888),\r\npart i, p. ii. Also see Crawfurd\xe2\x80\x99s Dictionary, pp. 238\xe2\x80\x93249.\r\n\r\nThe terms India Major (Greater India) and India Minor (Lesser India)\r\nare differently applied by different authors. Schiltbergen applied the\r\nterm Lesser India to the northern portion of the peninsula on this side\r\nof the Ganges, while the southern portion of the peninsula was termed\r\nGreater India. Marco Polo\xe2\x80\x99s Lesser India extended from Makran to and\r\nincluding the Coromandel coast, and his Greater India extended from the\r\nCoromandel coast to Cochin China, while Middle India was Abyssinia.\r\nMosto wrongly identifies India Major with the present Indian empire.\r\nSee Telfer\xe2\x80\x99s Johann Schiltberger (Hakluyt Society publications,\r\n1879). Friar Jordanus (Wonders of the East, Hakluyt Society edition,\r\nLondon, 1863), describes (pp. 11\xe2\x80\x9345) India the Less, India the\r\nGreater, and India Tertia. Yule points out that Jordanus\xe2\x80\x99s Lesser\r\nIndia embraces Sindh, and probably Mekran, and India along the coast as\r\nfar as some point immediately north of Malabar. Greater India extends\r\nfrom Malabar very indefinitely to the eastward, for he makes it include\r\nChampa. India Tertia is the east of Africa below Abyssinia. Thus\r\nJordanus just reverses the Lesser and Greater Indias of Marco Polo.\r\nRamusio who gives the Summary of Kingdoms of an old Portuguese\r\ngeographer, ends First India at Mangalore, and Second India at the\r\nGanges. Benjamin of Tudela speaks of \xe2\x80\x9cMiddle India which is called\r\nAden.\xe2\x80\x9d Conti divides India into three parts: the first extending from\r\nPersia to the Indus, the second from the Indus to the Ganges, and the\r\nthird all the land beyond. Pliny discusses whether Mekran and other\r\nlands belonged to India or Ariana.\r\n\r\n[274] MS. 5,650 adds: \xe2\x80\x9cand treat his subjects well.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n[275] This phrase is omitted in This shell is very white inside, while\r\nthe exterior is spotted a pale yellow color. It resembles\r\nmother-of-pearl, and is very common. Delgado says that most of the\r\nshellfish, are indigestible but highly esteemed. See Delgado\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nHistoria, p. 928.\r\n\r\n[303] MS. 5,650 adds: \xe2\x80\x9cWhich was of various strange kinds.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n[304] Eden says: \xe2\x80\x9cxvi. poundes weyght of iren.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n[305] MS. 5,650 reads: \xe2\x80\x9cThe captain-general did not wish to take too\r\ngreat a quantity of gold, so that the sailors might not sell their\r\nshare in the merchandise too cheaply, because of their lust for gold,\r\nand so that on that account he should not be constrained to do the same\r\nwith his merchandise, for he wished to sell it at as high a price as\r\npossible.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n[306] MS. 5,650 adds: \xe2\x80\x9cor any other balls\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n[307] MS. 5,650 makes the two armed men follow instead of precede the\r\nroyal banner.\r\n\r\n[308] MS. 5,650 adds: \xe2\x80\x9cand the natives of the country for their fear\r\nof it, fled hither and thither,\xe2\x80\x9d which is in place of the following\r\nsentence.\r\n\r\n[309] This sentence is omitted in MS. 5,650.\r\n\r\n[310] MS. 5,650 reads: \xe2\x80\x9cOne covered with red and the other with\r\nvelvet.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n[311] MS. 5,650 adds: \xe2\x80\x9cin the manner of the country.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n[312] The account of the baptism of the king is considerably abridged\r\nin MS. 5,650 where it reads as follows: \xe2\x80\x9cThen the captain began to\r\naddress the king through the interpreter, in order that he might incite\r\nhim to the faith of Jesus Christ. He told him that if he wished to\r\nbecome a good Christian (as he had signified on the preceding day),\r\nthat he must have all the idols of his country burned and set up a\r\ncross in their place, which they were all to adore daily on both knees,\r\nwith hands clasped and raised toward the heaven. The captain showed the\r\nking how he was to make the sign of the cross daily. In reply the king\r\nand all his men said that they would obey the captain\xe2\x80\x99s commandment,\r\nand do all that he told them. The captain took the king by the hand,\r\nand they walked to the platform. At his baptism the captain told the\r\nking that he would call him Dom Charles, after the emperor his\r\nsovereign. He named the prince Dom Fernand, after the brother of the\r\nsaid emperor, and the king of Mazzaua, Jehan. He gave the name of\r\nChristofle to the Moro, while he called each of the others by names\r\naccording to his fancy. Thus before the mass fifty men [sic: but an\r\nerror of the French adapter for five hundred] were baptized. At the\r\nconclusion of mass, the captain invited the king and the others of his\r\nchief men to dine with him, but he would not accept. However, he\r\naccompanied the captain to the shore, where, at his arrival, the ships\r\ndischarged all the artillery. Then embracing they took leave of one\r\nanother.\xe2\x80\x9d Eden gives the number baptized as five hundred men.\r\n\r\n[313] MS. 5,650 reads: \xe2\x80\x9cOn seeing that, she expressed the greatest\r\ndesire to became a Christian, and asking for baptism, she was baptized\r\nand given the name of Jehanne, after the emperor\xe2\x80\x99s mother.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n[314] There are many cases of this wholesale baptism in the history of\r\nthe Catholic missions in various countries, and it cannot be condemned\r\nentirely and regarded as devoid of good effects, for many instances\r\nreveal the contrary. See Jesuit Relations (Cleveland reissue).\r\n\r\n[315] Those last six words are omitted in MS. 5,650. Mosto conjectures\r\nthat solana means solecchio or solicchio signifying an apparatus to\r\nprotect one from the sun. Pigafetta may have misapplied the Spanish\r\nword solana, which signifies a place bathed by the noontide sun or a\r\nplace in which to take the sun.\r\n\r\n[316] This last clause is omitted in MS. 5,650.\r\n\r\n[317] MS. 5,650 adds: \xe2\x80\x9cand we gave it to her.\xe2\x80\x9d This was the image\r\nfound by one of Legazpi\xe2\x80\x99s soldiers in Ceb\xc3\xba in 1565 (see Vol. II, pp.\r\n120, 121, 128, 216, 217; and Vol. V, p. 41). Encarnaci\xc3\xb3n (Dic.\r\nbisaya-espa\xc3\xb1ol, Manila, 1851), says: \xe2\x80\x9cThe Cebuan Indians, both past\r\nand present, give the name of Bath\xc3\xa1la [God] to the image of the Holy\r\nChild, which is supposed to have been left by the celebrated\r\nMagallanes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n[318] MS. 5,650 reads: \xe2\x80\x9cevening.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n[319] MS. 5,650 mentions only the artillery. The \xe2\x80\x9ctromb\xe2\x80\x9d or\r\n\xe2\x80\x9ctrunk\xe2\x80\x9d was a kind of hand rocket-tube made of wood and hooped with\r\niron, and was used for discharging wild-fire or Greek-fire (see\r\nCorbett\xe2\x80\x99s Spanish War, 1585\xe2\x80\x9387 [London], 1898, p. 335). At this\r\npoint Stanley discontinues the narrative of MS. 5,650, and translates\r\nfrom Amoretti\xe2\x80\x99s version of the Italian MS.\r\n\r\n[320] MS. 5,650 reads: \xe2\x80\x9cto better instruct and confirm him in the\r\nfaith.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n[321] Eden says the queen was preceded by \xe2\x80\x9cthree younge damo\xc5\xbfelles\r\nand three men with theyr cappes in theyr handes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n[322] MS. 5,650 adds: \xe2\x80\x9cand presentation.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n[323] MS. 5,650 reads simply for this last clause: \xe2\x80\x9cand several\r\nothers,\xe2\x80\x9d omitting all the names.\r\n\r\n[324] MS. 5,650 reads: \xe2\x80\x9cand they all so swore.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n[325] MS. 5,650 reads from this point: \xe2\x80\x9cThen they swore, and thus the\r\ncaptain caused the king to swear by that image, by the life of the\r\nemperor his sovereign, and by his habit, to ever remain faithful and\r\nsubject to the emperor,\xe2\x80\x9d thus ascribing this oath to the king instead\r\nof to Magalh\xc3\xa3es. The words \xe2\x80\x9cby his habit\xe2\x80\x9d can refer only to\r\nMagalh\xc3\xa3es, who wore that of Santiago, and not to any habit worn by the\r\nbarbaric ruler of Ceb\xc3\xba.\r\n\r\n[326] MS. 5,650 adds: \xe2\x80\x9cand hang.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n[327] MS. 5,650 adds: \xe2\x80\x9cand deck.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n[328] MS. 5,650 adds: \xe2\x80\x9cand demolished.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n[329] MS. 5,650 adds: \xe2\x80\x9cand overthrew.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n[330] There is a strange difference between the Italian MS. and MS.\r\n5,650 in regard to these names. The latter reads to this point:\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere are a number of villages in that island, whose names and those\r\nof their chiefs are as follows: Cinghapola, Cilaton, Ciguibucan,\r\nCimaningha, Cimaticat, and Cicambul; another, Mandaui, and its chief\r\nand seignior, Lambuzzan; another Cot-cot, and its chief, Acibagalen;\r\nanother, Puzzo, and its chief, Apanoan; another, Lalan, and its chief,\r\nTheteu; another, Lulutan, and its chief, Tapan [Amoretti, followed by\r\nStanley, says Japau, and Mosto, Iapan]; another Cilumay; and also\r\nLubucun.\xe2\x80\x9d Amoretti, who places this list after the disastrous battle\r\nand consequent treachery of the Cebuans, and Stanley, have \xe2\x80\x9cLubucin:\r\nits chief is Cilumai.\xe2\x80\x9d Mandaui is Mandaue; Lalan may be Liloan;\r\nCot-cot is on the east coast; Lubucun may be Lub\xc3\xba, but Mosto (p. 78,\r\nnote 3) conjectures it to be Lambusan. An examination of the Nancy MS.\r\nmay reveal the source of this difference.\r\n\r\n[331] MS. 5,650 adds after the word borchies: \xe2\x80\x9cinstruments so\r\ncalled.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n[332] Probably cotton cloth. See Stanley\xe2\x80\x99s East African and Malabar\r\nCoasts, p. 65: \xe2\x80\x9cThey make there [i.e., in Cambay] many cloths of\r\nwhite cotton, fine and coarse, and other woven and colored fabrics, of\r\nall kinds and colours.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n[333] MS. 5,650 adds: \xe2\x80\x9cand closed.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n[334] MS. 5,650 reads: \xe2\x80\x9cShe who has killed the hog, puts a lighted\r\ntorch in her mouth, which she extinguishes, and which she holds\r\nconstantly alight with her teeth during that ceremony.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n[335] Cf. the ceremonies of the baylanes described by Loarca, Vol. V,\r\npp. 131, 133, and by Chirino, Vol. XII, p. 270.\r\n\r\n[336] Otorno: Mosto, p. 79, mistranscribes otoro, and queries Attorno\r\nin a note.\r\n\r\n[337] MS. 5,650 omits the description of this custom, giving only the\r\nfirst and last sentence to this point. Stanley omits the translation to\r\nthis point. See Vol. V, p. 117, and Vol. XVI, p. 130, where Loarca and\r\nMorga describe this custom.\r\n\r\n[338] Valzi: Mosto queries vasi, \xe2\x80\x9cjars,\xe2\x80\x9d which appears probable.\r\n\r\n[339] MS. 5,650 adds: \xe2\x80\x9cmade in the manner abovesaid;\xe2\x80\x9d but this was\r\ncrossed out, showing that the writer or adapter of that MS. had at\r\nfirst intended to narrate the custom that is given in the Italian MS.\r\n\r\n[340] This word is omitted in MS. 5,650.\r\n\r\n[341] MS. 5,650 reads: \xe2\x80\x9cThe other women sit about the dead chamber\r\nsadly and in tears.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n[342] Pigafetta uses the present and imperfect tenses rather\r\nindiscriminately throughout this narration, but we have translated\r\nuniformly in the present. Cf. Loarca\xe2\x80\x99s description of burial and\r\nmourning customs among the Visayans, Vol. V, pp. 129, 135, 137\xe2\x80\x93141;\r\nPlasencia\xe2\x80\x99s description among the Tag\xc3\xa1logs, Vol. VII, pp. 194, 195;\r\nand Morga, Vol. XVI, p. 133.\r\n\r\n[343] MS. 5,650 reads: \xe2\x80\x9cfive or six hours.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n[344] Eden in describing the island of Matan confuses the Pigafetta\r\nnarrative. He says: \xe2\x80\x9cNot farre from this Ilande of Zubut, is the Hand\r\nof Mathan, who\xc5\xbfe inhabitauntes v\xc5\xbfe maruelous ceremonies in theyr\r\n\xc5\xbfacrifices to the \xc5\xbfoone and burying the deade. They were rynges of\r\ngold abowt theyr priuie members.\xe2\x80\x9d In the description of the battle in\r\nMatan, Eden says that each of the three divisions of the islanders\r\ncontained \xe2\x80\x9ctwo thou\xc5\xbfand and fiftie men armed with bowes, arrowes,\r\ndartes and iauelins hardened at the poyntes with fyer.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n[345] To this point the Italian MS. and MS. 5,650 agree approximately.\r\nThe story of the battle in the latter MS., however, is much abridged\r\nand much less graphic. It is as follows: \xe2\x80\x9cThey replied that they had\r\nbamboo spears and stakes burned and hardened in the fire, and that we\r\ncould attack them when we wished. At daybreak, forty-nine of us leaped\r\ninto the water, in the place whither we had thus gone, at a distance of\r\nmore than three [sic] crossbow flights before we could reach shore, for\r\nthe boats could not approach nearer because of the rocks and reefs\r\nwhich were in the water. Thus we reached land, and attacked them. They\r\nwere arranged in three divisions, of more than one thousand five\r\nhundred persons. We shot many arrows at them from a distance, but it\r\nwas in vain, for they received them on their shields. They leaped\r\nhither and thither in such a way that scarce could we wound one of\r\nthem. On the other hand, our artillery in the boats was so far away\r\nfrom us that it could not aid us. Those people seeing that, and that\r\nthe captain had had some of their houses burned in order to inspire\r\nthem with terror, and having become more enraged, threw so many iron\r\npointed spears at us, and shot so many arrows even at the captain\r\nhimself that we could defend ourselves with difficulty. Finally, having\r\nbeen driven by them quite down to the shore, and while our captain was\r\nfighting bravely although wounded in the leg with an arrow, one of\r\nthose Indians hurled a poisoned bamboo lance into his face which laid\r\nhim stiff and dead. Then they pressed upon us so closely that we were\r\nforced to retire to our boats and to leave the dead body of the\r\ncaptain-general, with our other killed.\xe2\x80\x9d The eulogy on the dead\r\ncommander is approximately the same in both MSS., except at the end,\r\nwhere MS. 5,650 reads: \xe2\x80\x9cEight of our men died there with him, and\r\nfour Indians, who had become Christians. Of the enemy fifteen were\r\nkilled by the artillery of the ships, which had at last come to our\r\naid, while many of us were wounded.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBrito (Navarrete, iv, p. 308) says of the stay at Ceb\xc3\xba and the death\r\nof Magalh\xc3\xa3es: \xe2\x80\x9cThey stayed there about one month, and the majority\r\nof the people and the king became Christians. The king of Zub\xc3\xb3 ordered\r\nthe kings of the other islands to come to him, but inasmuch as two of\r\nthem refused to come, Magallanes, as soon as he learned it, resolved to\r\ngo to fight with them, and went to an island called Math\xc3\xa1. He set fire\r\nto a village, and not content with that, set out for a large\r\nsettlement, where he, his servant, and five Castilians were killed in\r\ncombat with the savages. The others, seeing their captain dead, went\r\nback to their boats.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n[346] Terciado: a Spanish word.\r\n\r\n[347] Carteava: a Spanish word.\r\n\r\n[348] The \xe2\x80\x9cRoteiro\xe2\x80\x9d (Stanley, p. 12) dates the battle April 28. The\r\naccount of the battle is as follows: \xe2\x80\x9cFernan de Magalh\xc3\xa3es desired\r\nthat the other kings, neighbours to this one, should become subject to\r\nthis who had become Christian: and these did not choose to yield such\r\nobedience. Fernan de Magalh\xc3\xa3es seeing that, got ready one night with\r\nhis boats, and burned the villages of those who would not yield the\r\nsaid obedience; and a matter of ten or twelve days after this was done,\r\nhe sent to a village about half a league from that which he had burned,\r\nwhich is named Matam, and which is also an island, and ordered them to\r\nsend him at once three goats, three pigs, three loads of rice, and\r\nthree loads of millet for provisions for the ships; they replied that\r\nfor each article which he sent to ask them three of, they would send to\r\nhim by twos, and if he was satisfied with this they would at once\r\ncomply, if not, it might be as he pleased, but that they would not give\r\nit. Because they did not choose to grant what he demanded of them,\r\nFernan de Magalh\xc3\xa3es ordered three boats to be equipped with a matter\r\nof fifty or sixty men, and went against the said place, which was on\r\nthe 28th day of April, in the morning; there they found many people,\r\nwho might well be as many as three thousand or four thousand men, who\r\nfought with such a good will that the said Fernan de Magalh\xc3\xa3es was\r\nkilled there, with six of his men, in the year 1521.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n[349] Navarrete (iv, pp. 65, 66) gives the names of the men killed with\r\nMagalh\xc3\xa3es on April 27 as follows: Christ\xc3\xb3bal Rabelo, then captain of\r\nthe \xe2\x80\x9cVictoria;\xe2\x80\x9d Francisco Espinosa, a sailor; Anton Gallego, a\r\ncommon seaman; Juan de Torres, sobresaliente and soldier; Rodrigo\r\nNieto, servant of Juan de Cartagena; Pedro Gomez, servant of Gonzalo\r\nEspinosa; and Anton de Escovar, sobresaliente, wounded but died April\r\n29.\r\n\r\n[350] See Vol. I, pp. 325, 326, note 215*.\r\n\r\n[351] MS. 5,650 gives this name as Duart Bobase, although lower it is\r\nspelled Barbase. Duarte or Odoardo Barbosa, the son of Diogo Barbosa,\r\nwho after serving in Portugal, became alcaide of the Sevilla arsenal,\r\nwas born at Lisbon at the end of the fifteenth century. He spent the\r\nyears 1501\xe2\x80\x931516 in the Orient, the result of that stay being his\r\nLivro emque d\xc3\xa0 rela\xc3\xa7\xc3\xa3o do que viu e ouviu no Oriente, which was\r\nfirst published at Lisbon in 1813 in vol. vii of Collec\xc3\xa7ao de noticias\r\npara a historia et geographia das na\xc3\xa7\xc3\xb5es ultramarinas, and its\r\ntranslation by Stanley, A description of the coasts of East Africa and\r\nMalabar (Hakluyt Society publications, London, 1866). He became a clerk\r\nin the Portuguese factory at Cananor under his uncle Gil Fernandez\r\nBarbosa, and became so expert in the Malabar language that he was said\r\nto speak it even better than the natives. On account of his facility in\r\nthe language he had been appointed commissioner by Nuno da Cunha to\r\nnegotiate peace with the Zamorin. He was commissioned in 1515 to\r\noversee the construction of some galleys by Alboquerque. While at\r\nSevilla, Magalh\xc3\xa3es lived in the household of Diogo Barbosa, where he\r\nmarried Duarte\xe2\x80\x99s sister Beatriz. Duarte embarked on the\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTrinidad\xe2\x80\x9d as a sobresaliente, and it was he who captured the\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVictoria\xe2\x80\x9d from the mutineers at Port St. Julian, after which he\r\nbecame captain of that vessel. Failing to recover Magalh\xc3\xa3es body from\r\nthe natives of Mact\xc3\xa1n, he was himself slain at Ceb\xc3\xba at the fatal\r\nbanquet May 1, 1521. Besides the above book, which is a most valuable\r\ncontribution to early Oriental affairs, there is extant in the Torre do\r\nTombo a letter written by him from Cananor, January 12, 1513,\r\ncomplaining of the Portuguese excesses. See Guillemard\xe2\x80\x99s Magellan;\r\nStanley\xe2\x80\x99s Vasco da Gama; Birch\xe2\x80\x99s Alboquerque; and Hoefer\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nNouvelle Biographie G\xc3\xa9n\xc3\xa9rale (Paris, 1855)\r\n\r\n[352] See ante, note 147.\r\n\r\n[353] Magalh\xc3\xa3es married Beatriz Barbosa, daughter of Diogo Barbosa in\r\nSevilla, probably in the year 1517. One son Rodrigo was born of the\r\nunion, who was about six months old at the time of the departure.\r\nRodrigo died in September, 1521, and in the March following Beatriz\r\ndied. See Guillemard, ut supra, pp. 89\xe2\x80\x9391, 322.\r\n\r\n[354] MS. 5,650 adds: \xe2\x80\x9cand to advise the Christian king.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n[355] Mosto transcribes this word wrongly as facente, \xe2\x80\x9cbusy.\xe2\x80\x9d MS.\r\n5,650 reads: \xe2\x80\x9cwiser and more affectionate than before.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n[356] MS. 5,650 adds: \xe2\x80\x9cand presents.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n[357] The constable was Gonzalo Gomez de Espinosa, who was left behind\r\nwith the \xe2\x80\x9cTrinidad\xe2\x80\x9d and was one of the four survivors of that\r\nill-fated vessel, returning to Spain long after.\r\n\r\n[358] This sentence is confused in MS. 5,650, reading: jehan Caruaie\r\nauecques le bari\xc5\xbfel \xc5\xbfen retourner\xe1\xba\xbdt qui nous dirent comment jlz\r\nauoyent veu mener celluy quy fut guery par miracle et le pre\xc5\xbftre a \xc5\xbfa\r\nmai\xc5\xbfon et que pour cela jlz \xc5\xbfen e\xc5\xbftoyent partiz eulx doubtans de\r\nquelque male aduanture. By dropping the first et this becomes\r\nequivalent to the text.\r\n\r\n[359] MS. 5,650 reads: \xe2\x80\x9cfor we would kill him.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n[360] MS. 5,650 reads: \xe2\x80\x9cBut Jehan Carvaie, his comrade, and others\r\nrefused, for fear lest they would not remain masters there if the boat\r\nwent ashore.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIn regard to Jo\xc3\xa3o Serr\xc3\xa3o\xe2\x80\x99s death, Brito (Navarrete, iv, p. 309)\r\nsays: \xe2\x80\x9cAs soon as the men in the ships saw that slaughter, they\r\nhoisted their anchors, and tried to set sail in order to return to\r\nBurneo. At that juncture, the savages brought Juan Serrano, one of\r\nthose whom they wished to ransom, and asked two guns and two bahars of\r\ncopper for him, besides some Brittanias or linens such as they carried\r\nin the ships as merchandise of trade and barter. Serrano told them to\r\ntake him to the ship and he would give them what they asked, but they,\r\non the contrary, insisted that those things be taken ashore. But [the\r\nmen in the ships] fearing another act of treachery like the past, set\r\nsail, and abandoned that man there, and nothing more was heard of\r\nhim.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n[361] The \xe2\x80\x9cRoteiro\xe2\x80\x9d (Stanley, p. 13) says nothing about the\r\nbanquet, but says that the men, twenty-eight in number, counting the\r\ntwo captains, went ashore to ask pilots to Borneo, whereupon the\r\nnatives, who had determined upon their course of action attacked and\r\nkilled them. Peter Martyr (Mosto, p. 81, note 5) asserts that the\r\nviolation of the women by the sailors was the cause of the massacre.\r\nConcerning the number killed, Brito (Navarrete, iv, p. 309) says that\r\nthirty-five or thirty-six men went ashore, and Castanheda and Gomara\r\nsay thirty, the last asserting that a like number were made slaves, of\r\nwhom eight were sold in China. Peter Martyr places the number of the\r\nslain at twelve. Navarrete (iv, pp. 66, 67) gives the names of those\r\nmassacred as follows:\r\n\r\n\r\n    Duarte Barbosa            captain of the \xe2\x80\x9cTrinidad\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n    Juan Serrano              captain of the \xe2\x80\x9cConcepcion\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n    Luis Alfonso de Gois      captain of the \xe2\x80\x9cVictoria\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n    Andres de S. Martin       pilot of his Majesty\r\n    Sancho de Heredia         notary\r\n    Leon de Ezpeleta          notary\r\n    Pedro de Valderrama       priest\r\n    Francisco Martin          cooper\r\n    Simon de la Rochela       calker\r\n    Crist\xc3\xb3bal Rodriguez       steward\r\n    Francisco de Madrid       sobresaliente and soldier\r\n    Hernando de Aguilar       servant of Luis de Mendoza\r\n    Guillermo Fenesi or\r\n      Tanagu\xc3\xac                 gunner of the \xe2\x80\x9cTrinidad\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n    Anton Rodriguez           sailor\r\n    Juan Sigura               sailor\r\n    Francisco Picora          sailor\r\n    Francisco Martin          sailor\r\n    Anton de Goa              common seaman\r\n    Rodrigo de Hurrira        common seaman\r\n    Pedro Herrero             sobresaliente\r\n    Hartiga                   sobresaliente\r\n    Juan de Silva, Portuguese sobresaliente\r\n    Nu\xc3\xb1o                      servant of Magallanes\r\n    Henrique, from M\xc3\xa1laca     servant of Magallanes and interpreter\r\n    Peti Juan, French         servant of Magallanes\r\n    Francisco de la Mezquita  servant of Magallanes\r\n    Francisco                 son-in-law of Juan Serrano\r\n\r\n\r\nAll of these names are to be found in Navarrete\xe2\x80\x99s list. See ante,\r\nnote 26.\r\n\r\n[362] Chiacare: the nangca; see Vol. XXXIV, p. 107, where Pigafetta\r\ndescribes and names this fruit. Mosto confuses it with the durio\r\nxibethenus, which is abundant in the western islands of the Indian\r\narchipelagoes, Mindanao being the only one of the Philippines where it\r\nis found (Crawfurd, Dictionary); but it is the Artocarpus integrifolia\r\n(see Vol. XVI, p. 88, note 72). MS. 5,650 makes this \xe2\x80\x9ccapers.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n[363] MS. 5,650 omits mention of the panicum, sorgo, garlic, and\r\nnangcas.\r\n\r\n[364] MS. 5,650 reads: \xe2\x80\x9cone to the east northeast, and the other to\r\nthe west southwest.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n[365] MS. 5,650 adds: \xe2\x80\x9cand eleven minutes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n[366] Stanley says wrongly 154\xc2\xb0.\r\n\r\n[367] This word ends a page in the original Italian MS. On the\r\nfollowing page is a repetition of the title: Vocabili deli populi\r\ngentilli, that is \xe2\x80\x9cWords of those heathen peoples.\xe2\x80\x9d MS. 5,650 does\r\nnot contain this list, and it is also omitted by Stanley.\r\n\r\n[368] See ante, note 160.\r\n\r\n[369] Bassag bassag does not correspond to \xe2\x80\x9cshin,\xe2\x80\x9d but to \xe2\x80\x9cbasket\r\nfor holding clothes, etc.,\xe2\x80\x9d or \xe2\x80\x9ccartilage of the nose;\xe2\x80\x9d or\r\npossibly to basac basac, \xe2\x80\x9cthe sound made by falling water.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n[370] The equivalent of Pigafetta\xe2\x80\x99s dana is daoa or daua,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cmillet.\xe2\x80\x9d Mais, probably the equivalent of humas is the word for\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cpanicum.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n[371] Tahil is found in the Tag\xc3\xa1log dictionaries, and is the name of a\r\nspecific weight, not weight in general. It is the Chinese weight called\r\n\xe2\x80\x9ctael,\xe2\x80\x9d which was introduced by the Chinese into the East Indies,\r\nwhence it spread throughout the various archipelagoes. See Crawfurd\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nDictionary; and Vols. III, p. 192, note 57; IV, p. 100, note 11; and\r\nVII, p. 88.\r\n\r\n[372] See Note 582, post.\r\n\r\n[373] Tinapay (used also by the Bicols to denote any kind of bread)\r\ndenotes a kind of cake or loaf made with flour and baked about the size\r\nof a chocolate-cup saucer. Two of these are put together before baking\r\nwith some sugar between. The word is extended also to wheat bread and\r\nto the hosts. See Encarnaci\xc3\xb3n\xe2\x80\x99s Diccionario.\r\n\r\n[374] Amoretti\xe2\x80\x99s conjectured reading of sonaglio\r\n(\xe2\x80\x9chawk\xe2\x80\x99s-bell\xe2\x80\x9d) for conaglio (see Mosto, p. 83), proves correct\r\nfrom the Visayan dictionaries.\r\n\r\n[375] Baloto signifies a canoe dug out of a single log. One of twenty\r\nvaras in length is termed bilis, while the hull alone is called\r\ndal\xc3\xa1mas.\r\n\r\n[376] Most of the words of Pigafetta\xe2\x80\x99s Visayan vocabulary can be\r\ndistinguished in the dictionaries of that language, although it is\r\nnecessary to make allowance at times for Pigafetta\xe2\x80\x99s Italian phonetic\r\nrendering. Following is a list of the words that can be distinguished\r\nfrom Diccionario bisaya-espa\xc3\xb1ol y espa\xc3\xb1ol-bisaya (Manila, 1885), by\r\nJuan F\xc3\xa9lix de la Encarnaci\xc3\xb3n, O.S.A. (Recollect); and Diccionario\r\nHispano-bisaya y bisaya-espa\xc3\xb1ol (Manila, 1895) by Antonio Sanchez de\r\nla Rosa, O.S.F. See also Pocket dictionary of the English, Spanish and\r\nVisayan languages (Cebu, 1900) by H. M. Cohen; and Mallat\xe2\x80\x99s Les\r\nPhilippines (Paris, 1846), ii, pp. 175\xe2\x80\x93238. yssida \xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94; isda       \xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94; isda\r\na colored fish  panapsapan   panaps\xc3\xa1pan     panapsapan\r\na red fish      timuan       \xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94             tiao (?)\r\nanother fish    pilax        \xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94             pilas\r\nship            benaoa       b\xc3\xa1ngca         \xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\r\nking            raia         hari           hadi\r\none             uzza         us\xc3\xa1            usa\r\ntwo             dua          doha           duha\r\nthree           tolo         tol\xc3\xb3           tolo\r\nfour            upat         op\xc3\xa1t           upat\r\nfive            lima         lim\xc3\xa1           lima\r\nsix             onom         on\xc3\xb3m           unum\r\nseven           pitto        pit\xc3\xb3           pito\r\neight           gualu        oal\xc3\xb3           ualo\r\nnine            ciam         si\xc3\xa0m           siam\r\nten             polo         napol\xc3\xb3         napolo\r\n\r\n\r\nSome of the words present difficulties however, due probably to error\r\non Pigafetta\xe2\x80\x99s part and the obstacles in the method of communication\r\nbetween peoples the genius of whose respective languages is entirely\r\ndistinct. The general Visayan word for \xe2\x80\x9cman\xe2\x80\x9d is tao or tauo,\r\nalthough Mallat gives a form dala, which may correspond to the lac of\r\nPigafetta (but see Vol. V, p. 123, where the origin of the words lalac,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cman,\xe2\x80\x9d and babaye, \xe2\x80\x9cwoman,\xe2\x80\x9d are given by Loarca). Babaye\r\n(babae) is the general word for \xe2\x80\x9cwoman\xe2\x80\x9d or \xe2\x80\x9cmarried woman;\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nwhile binibini is given by Mallat as the Tag\xc3\xa1log equivalent of\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cgirl,\xe2\x80\x9d and by Santos in his Vocabulario de la lengua tagala\r\n(Manila, 1835) as the equivalent of \xe2\x80\x9cinfluential woman.\xe2\x80\x9d Liog is\r\nused for both \xe2\x80\x9cthroat\xe2\x80\x9d and \xe2\x80\x9cneck.\xe2\x80\x9d Tian is properly\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cbelly,\xe2\x80\x9d and the mistake would arise naturally in Pigafetta\r\npointing to himself when desiring the word for \xe2\x80\x9cbody,\xe2\x80\x9d which would\r\nbe construed by the natives to that particular part toward which he\r\nhappened to point. Boto is used for both the male and female generative\r\norgans, especially the latter, as well as for the testicles. Britiis\r\ncorresponds to both \xe2\x80\x9cshin\xe2\x80\x9d and \xe2\x80\x9ccalf of the leg.\xe2\x80\x9d Iro denotes\r\nalso the civet cat. Bulan the equivalent of Pigafetta\xe2\x80\x99s bolon is the\r\nword for \xe2\x80\x9cmoon\xe2\x80\x9d instead of \xe2\x80\x9cstar.\xe2\x80\x9d The occurrence of what are\r\ntoday Tag\xc3\xa1log forms in Pigafetta\xe2\x80\x99s list shows how the various\r\ndialects shade into one another and how the one has retained words that\r\nhave sunk into disuse in the other.\r\n\r\n[377] Preceding this paragraph in the Italian MS. (folio 38b) is the\r\nchart of the island of Panilonghon (Panisonghon; q.v., p. 202). It is\r\ngiven on folio 51a of MS. 5,650, preceded by the words: \xe2\x80\x9cBelow is\r\nshown the islands of Panilonghon.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n[378] The \xe2\x80\x9cRoteiro\xe2\x80\x9d (Stanley, pp. 13, 14) says that the captains\r\nelected in place of those killed at Ceb\xc3\xba were \xe2\x80\x9cJoam Lopez\r\n[Carvalho], who was the chief treasurer\xe2\x80\x9d to \xe2\x80\x9cbe captain-major of\r\nthe fleet, and the chief constable of the fleet\xe2\x80\x9d to \xe2\x80\x9cbe captain of\r\none of the ships; he was named Gonzalo Vaz Despinosa.\xe2\x80\x9d Pigafetta\r\nmakes no mention at all of Elcano, who brought the \xe2\x80\x9cVictoria\xe2\x80\x9d home;\r\nboth the above captains remaining with the \xe2\x80\x9cTrinidad.\xe2\x80\x9d When the\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cConcepcion\xe2\x80\x9d was burned, only one hundred and fifteen men were left\r\nfor the working of the two ships (see Guillemard, ut supra, p. 267),\r\nalthough the \xe2\x80\x9cRoteiro\xe2\x80\x9d (Stanley, p. 14) says one hundred and eight\r\nmen, and Barros, one hundred and eighty.\r\n\r\n[379] In Eden: \xe2\x80\x9cPauiloghon, where they founde blacke men lyke vnto\r\nthe Sara\xc5\xbfins.\xe2\x80\x9d This is the island of Panglao and the \xe2\x80\x9cblack men\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nare the Negritos. See W. A. Reed\xe2\x80\x99s Negritos of Zambales, published by\r\nDepartment of the Interior \xe2\x80\x9cEthnological Survey Publications\xe2\x80\x9d ii,\r\npart i (Manila, 1904), which says (p. 20) that the only large islands,\r\nbesides Luz\xc3\xb3n, inhabited at present by Negritos are Panay, Negros,\r\nMindanao, and Paragua, although they do inhabit some of the smaller\r\nislands. The pure type is decreasing through marriage with the Bukidnon\r\nor mountain Visayans; and (p. 22) \xe2\x80\x9cso far there is no evidence that\r\nNegritos exist on Cebu, Bohol, Samar, and Leyte. The Negrito population\r\nof the Philippines is probably not in excess of 25,000. The U. S.\r\ncensus report of 1900 gives to Panglao a population of 14,347, all\r\ncivilized. See also Census of the Philippines, i, pp. 411, 415, 436,\r\n468, 478, 532, 533.\r\n\r\n[380] MS. 5,650 reads: \xe2\x80\x9cWhen entering that house, we were preceded by\r\nmany reed and palmleaf torches.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n[381] These two words are omitted in MS. 5,650.\r\n\r\n[382] See Crawfurd\xe2\x80\x99s Dictionary, pp. 368, 369, on the origin and use\r\nof rice in the eastern islands, and the etymology of the native names\r\nfor that grain; and Census of the Philippines, iv.\r\n\r\n[383] Instead of this last clause, MS. 5,650 reads: \xe2\x80\x9cwhere he slept\r\nwith his principal wife.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n[384] MS. 5,650 reads: \xe2\x80\x9cin the houses of the king.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n[385] MS. 5,650 reads: \xe2\x80\x9clittle valleys.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n[386] Cf. Vol. III, pp. 56, 57.\r\n\r\n[387] MS. 5,650 reads: \xe2\x80\x9cboat.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n[388] MS. 5,650 reads: \xe2\x80\x9cCalanoa;\xe2\x80\x9d and Eden: \xe2\x80\x9cCalauar.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n[389] MS. 5,650 reads: \xe2\x80\x9cone hundred and sixty-six;\xe2\x80\x9d and Eden:\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c170.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n[390] Albo (Navarrete, iv, p. 221) reads as follows when relating the\r\ncourse of the ships on leaving Ceb\xc3\xba: \xe2\x80\x9cWe left Subu and sailed\r\nsouthwest to a latitude of 9 and three-fourths degrees, between the end\r\nof Subu and an island called Bohol. Toward the western end of Subu lies\r\nanother island, by name, Panilongo, which is inhabited by blacks. That\r\nisland and Subu contain gold and considerable ginger. The former lies\r\nin 9 and one-third degrees and Subu in 10 and one-third degrees.\r\nAccordingly we left that channel and went 10 leguas south and anchored\r\nin the island of Bohol. There we made two ships of the three, burning\r\nthe third, because we had no men. The last-named island lies in 9 and\r\none-half degrees. We left Bohol and sailed southwest toward Quipit, and\r\nanchored at that settlement on the right hand side of a river. On the\r\nnorthwest and open side are two islets which lie in 8 and one-half\r\ndegrees. We could get no food there, for the people had none, but we\r\nmade peace with them. That island of Quipit contains a quantity of\r\ngold, ginger, and cinnamon. Accordingly, we determined to go in search\r\nof food. The distance from the headland of Quipit to the first islands\r\nis about 112 leguas. It and the islands lie in an east by north and\r\nsouth by west direction; and this island [i.e., Mindanao] extends quite\r\ngenerally east and west.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe \xe2\x80\x9cRoteiro\xe2\x80\x9d (Stanley, p. 14) calls the port of Quipit (which is\r\nlocated on the northeastern coast of Mindanao) Capyam or Quype.\r\nCarvalho gave the boat of the burned ship to the inhabitants of that\r\nplace. Brito (Navarrete, iv, p. 309) says that they learned the\r\nlocation of Borneo at Mindanao. Quipit becomes Gibith in Transylvanus,\r\nChipico in Peter Martyr, and Quepindo in Barros (see Mosto, p. 84, note\r\n2).\r\n\r\n[391] The first European mention of the island of Luz\xc3\xb3n. Luz\xc3\xb3n is\r\nderived from the Malay l\xc3\xa2sung (Tagalog, losong), \xe2\x80\x9cmortar.\xe2\x80\x9d See\r\nCrawfurd\xe2\x80\x99s Dictionary, pp. 222, 223.\r\n\r\n[392] Pigafetta evidently means the Chinese by the Lequians who are\r\nknown to have carried on trade for many years with the Philippines, and\r\nwho indeed, once owned them.\r\n\r\nFollowing this paragraph in the Italian MS. (folio 40a) is the chart of\r\nCaghaiam (q.v., p. 202). This chart is shown on folio 53b in MS. 5,650,\r\npreceded by the words: \xe2\x80\x9cBelow is shown the island of Caghaian.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n[393] MS. 5,650 does not mention the cuirasses.\r\n\r\n[394] Eden reads: \xe2\x80\x9c40. leagues.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n[395] Albo (Navarrete, iv, p. 221) says: \xe2\x80\x9cWe left that place [i.e.,\r\nQuipit] and sailed west southwest, southwest, and west, until we came\r\nto an island containing very few inhabitants and called Quagayan. We\r\nanchored in the northern part of that island, where we asked for the\r\nlocation of the island of Poluan, in order to get provisions of rice,\r\nfor that island contains it in abundance, and many ships are laden\r\nthere for other districts. Accordingly we sailed west northwest and\r\ncame across the headland of the island of Poluan.\xe2\x80\x9d The \xe2\x80\x9cRoteiro\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n(Stanley, p. 14) calls Cagaiam, Caram. It is the island of Cagayan\r\nSulu, which lies northeast of Borneo.\r\n\r\n[396] The \xe2\x80\x9cRoteiro\xe2\x80\x9d (Stanley, p. 15) says that the ships contained\r\nonly sufficient provisions for a week.\r\n\r\n[397] Eden reads: \xe2\x80\x9cC.lxxix. degrees and a third parte.\xe2\x80\x9d MS. 5,650\r\nreads: \xe2\x80\x9cone hundred and sixty-one and one-third degrees.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n[398] Occurrences at Palawan are given as follows by Albo (Navarrete,\r\niv, pp. 221, 222): \xe2\x80\x9cThen we sailed north by east along the coast [of\r\nPalawan] until we reached a village called Saocao, where we made peace.\r\nIts inhabitants were Moros. We went to another village of Cafres, where\r\nwe bartered for a considerable quantity of rice, and consequently laid\r\nin a good supply of provisions. That coast extends northeast and\r\nsouthwest. The headland of its northeastern part lies in 9 and\r\none-third degrees, and that of the southwestern part in 8 and one-third\r\ndegrees. Then on returning to the southwest quite to the headland of\r\nthis island, we found an island near which is a bay. In this course and\r\nalong Poluan many shoals are found. This headland lies east and west\r\nwith Quipit and northeast by east and southwest by west with\r\nQuagayan.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe \xe2\x80\x9cRoteiro\xe2\x80\x9d (Stanley, pp. 15\xe2\x80\x9317) gives a fuller account of\r\noccurrences at Palawan. At the first settlement at which they attempt\r\nto land, the natives prove hostile, whereupon they go toward another\r\nisland, but contrary weather compelling them to anchor near Palawan,\r\nthey are invited ashore on that island by the people of another\r\nvillage. There one of the soldiers, Joam de Campos, lands alone in\r\norder to get provisions. Being received kindly at this port, named\r\nDyguasam (perhaps Puerto Princesa), the people set about preparing\r\nprovisions for the strangers. Then going to another nearby village,\r\nwhere Carvalho makes peace with the chief, provisions of rice, goats,\r\nand swine are bought. At the latter village, a Portuguese-speaking\r\nnegro who has been baptized at the Moluccas, is met, who promises to\r\nguide them to Borneo, but he fails them at the last moment. Capturing a\r\nprau and three Moros near the former village, they are guided to\r\nBorneo. Brito (Navarrete, iv, p. 309) says that the two ships remained\r\na month in Palawan, \xe2\x80\x9ca rich country, where they got new directions\r\nabout Burneo, and captured two men to guide them there.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAt this point in the Italian MS. (folio 41a) follows the chart of\r\nSundan and Pulaoam (q.v., p. 210). MS. 5,650 shows it on folio 54b,\r\nwhere it is preceded by the words: \xe2\x80\x9cChart of the island of Pulaoan\r\nand the port of Tegozzao.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n[399] MS. 5,650 reads: \xe2\x80\x9call.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n[400] This passage is defective in MS. 5,650, where it reads as\r\nfollows: \xe2\x80\x9cThey have bows with wooden arrows more than one palmo long,\r\nsome of which are pointed with long sharp fishbones, poisoned with\r\npoisonous herbs, while others are tipped with poisoned bamboo.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n[401] MS. 5,650 reads: \xe2\x80\x9cmace.\xe2\x80\x9d Jannetone as pointed out by Mosto\r\n(p. 85, note 4) was a missile weapon.\r\n\r\n[402] Cockfighting is still the great diversion of the Malays and\r\nMalasian peoples.
